Now we need a rigorous proof.

Given the difficulty, maybe we should attempt an analytic proof with coordinates, but try to simplify using algebraic manipulations. Since we have parameters m,n,h, and r,R defined in terms of them, we can compute everything in terms of m,n,h. The expressions might simplify nicely if we use the relationships.

Let’s set:

- Let M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with 0 < m < n.
- Let A = (0,h) with h>0.
- Then r = MA = √(m^2+h^2), R = NA = √(n^2+h^2).
- C = (m - r, 0) because on Ω on the ray from M opposite N. So C_x = m - r.
- D = (n + R, 0) because on Γ on the ray from N opposite M. So D_x = n + R.
- P is circumcenter of ACD. Since C and D have y=0, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line through midpoint: x = ( (m - r) + (n + R) )/2 = (m + n - r + R)/2. So p_x = (m + n - r + R)/2.
- p_y is found from perpendicular bisector of AC. A=(0,h), C=(m - r,0). Midpoint AC: ((m - r)/2, h/2). Slope AC: (0 - h)/( (m - r) - 0 ) = -h/(m - r) = h/(r - m) (since m - r negative, r - m positive). So perpendicular slope = -(r - m)/h. So line: y - h/2 = -(r - m)/h (x - (m - r)/2). At x = p_x, we get y = h/2 - (r - m)/h (p_x - (m - r)/2). Compute p_x - (m - r)/2 = (m+n - r + R)/2 - (m - r)/2 = (n + R)/2. So p_y = h/2 - (r - m)(n+R)/(2h). So p_y = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R))/(2h). This is correct.

Now define v = P - A = (p_x, p_y - h). Compute p_y - h = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R))/(2h) - h = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R) - 2h^2)/(2h) = -(h^2 + (r - m)(n+R))/(2h). So v = (p_x, - (h^2 + (r - m)(n+R))/(2h) ). Let’s denote T = h^2 + (r - m)(n+R). Then v = (p_x, -T/(2h)). So v_y negative.

Now we need E and F. As derived, t_E = (2m p_x + T)/|v|^2, t_F = (2n p_x + T)/|v|^2, where |v|^2 = p_x^2 + T^2/(4h^2). But we might not need explicit t_E, t_F; we can compute E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. So E = (t_E p_x, h - (t_E T)/(2h)), F = (t_F p_x, h - (t_F T)/(2h)). Since t_E, t_F are scalars. Also note that B = (0,-h).

Now we want circle through B, E, F. Let's denote coordinates:

B = (0, -h)
E = (x_E, y_E) with x_E = t_E p_x, y_E = h - (t_E T)/(2h)
F = (x_F, y_F) with x_F = t_F p_x, y_F = h - (t_F T)/(2h)

We also have that t_E and t_F satisfy: from earlier, t_E = (2m p_x + T)/|v|^2, t_F = (2n p_x + T)/|v|^2. So these are known.

Now we need to show that line L: through H parallel to v (direction v) is tangent to circle (BEF). H = (p_x, (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/p_y). Note p_y is negative.

We can try to compute the equation of circle (BEF) and then compute the condition that the distance from its center to line L equals its radius. But maybe we can find a more elegant condition: For a given line L through H, L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but that's not directly.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that if we take a line through H with direction v, it is tangent if and only if the line is perpendicular to the radius at the point of tangency. Actually, if we param: (x,y) = A + t v. Then |A + t v|^2 = r^2 gives t=0 and t = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2. So E = A - 2 (A·v)/|v|^2 v. Similarly, F = A - 2 ((A-c)·v)/|v|^2 v.

Now B = (a, -h) (conjugate of A). So we have three points B, E, F.

We want to show that line L through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF). H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M=(0,0), N=(c,0), P=(p, y_P). As derived, H = (p, p(c-p)/y_P). Since y_P is negative, H is below x-axis.

Now we need to prove that the distance from the circumcenter O of triangle BEF to line L equals the radius of (BEF). That is a condition that can be expressed as: the line L is tangent to circle (BEF). Equivalent to: the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but that's not helpful.

Another way: Show that the line L is the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF) and that A lies on the circle? No.

Perhaps we can compute the equation of circle (BEF) in terms of A, B, and the line AP. Since E and F are on line AP, we can find the equation of the circle through B and the two points on line AP. Let the line AP have equation: l: y = α x + β (with α = (y_P-h)/(p-a)? Actually we can find α,β from A and P. But maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter from the midpoint of BH to something? Or that the center O is the midpoint of the segment joining the midpoints of BH and something? In numeric, O = (4.51,-9.605). H=(4.915,-0.255). B=(0,-3). Midpoint of BH = (2.4575,-3.1275). That's far.

Maybe we can find that O is the reflection of P across the midpoint of something? Not.

Given the difficulty, perhaps we should try to use vector geometry with coordinates and simplify the algebra using the known relations. Let's attempt to compute the circle (BEF) directly using coordinates and then compute the distance from its center to line L. We can try to derive a condition that the line L is tangent if and only if some expression is zero, and then verify that expression holds identically using the relations. This will be a brute-force algebraic verification. Since the problem is from an olympiad, the solution likely uses synthetic reasoning, but we can produce an analytic proof that is rigorous and maybe simpler. However, we need to present it clearly.

Let's set up coordinates with M=(0,0), N=(c,0), A=(a,h) with h>0, and the relations:

(1) a^2 + h^2 = r^2.
(2) (a-c)^2 + h^2 = R^2.
We also have r < R, so c > 0? Actually from (2) we have R^2 = (a-c)^2+h^2 = a^2-2ac+c^2+h^2 = r^2 -2ac+c^2 => 2ac = r^2 - R^2 + c^2 => a = (c^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2c). So a is determined by c,r,R. And h^2 = r^2 - a^2. Since r<R, a may be positive or negative? In our numeric, a=-4, so negative. So a could be negative. But we can keep as is.

Now we have C = (-r,0), D = (c+R,0). Then P = (p, y_P) with p = (c+R-r)/2, y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). So y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). Also, note that (c+R-r) = 2p.

Now we need to compute E and F. Let v = (v_x, v_y) = P - A = (p - a, y_P - h). Compute v_x = p - a = (c+R-r)/2 - a. v_y = y_P - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h. We'll keep as v.

Now compute A·v = a v_x + h v_y. But we might not need that explicitly. Instead, we can find E and F using the chord property: For line through A, the other intersection with circle centered at origin is given by: E = (r^2 / |A|^2) * A? That's for a circle centered at origin and a line through A? Actually, if we have a circle centered at origin, and a line through point A on the circle, the second intersection E is given by: E = (r^2 / |A|^2) * A? That would be A itself because |A|=r, so (r^2/|A|^2)=1, so E=A. That's not right. Wait, there is a formula: For a circle centered at origin, if a line through A (on circle) has direction v, then the other intersection is given by: E = A - 2 (A·v)/|v|^2 v. That's correct.

Alternatively, we can use the concept of the chord: The reflection of A across the line through the origin perpendicular to the chord? Not.

Maybe we can find a more convenient parametrization: Since we have a line through A, we can write the line equation in terms of its angle. But maybe we can compute the coordinates of E and F using the fact that A, E, and the center are related by the chord. For circle centered at origin, if A is on circle, then the chord AE is such that the origin is the circumcenter, so the line through origin and the midpoint of AE is perpendicular to AE. So the midpoint of AE is the projection of the origin onto the line through A and E? Actually, the perpendicular from the origin to chord AE goes through the midpoint of AE. So the midpoint M_AE is the foot of the perpendicular from origin to line AP. So we can compute M_AE = foot of perpendicular from O to line AP. That foot is given by: M_AE = A + t0 v, where t0 = - (A·v)/|v|^2? Because the projection of A onto the line direction v gives the foot from origin? Wait, the foot of perpendicular from origin to line through A with direction v is the point on line that minimizes distance to origin. That point is given by: A + t0 v, where t0 = - (A·v)/|v|^2. Indeed, because we want (A + t v) ⟂ v? Actually, the condition for foot from origin: (A + t v) is perpendicular to v? No, the vector from origin to point on line is A + t v; we want that vector to be perpendicular to the line direction v, so (A + t v) · v = 0 => A·v + t |v|^2 = 0 => t = - (A·v)/|v|^2. So foot from origin to line is point F0 = A - (A·v)/|v|^2 v. Then the midpoint of AE is that foot? Check: For a chord of a circle centered at origin, the line from origin to the midpoint of chord is perpendicular to chord, so the midpoint is the foot of the perpendicular from origin to the chord line. So yes, midpoint of AE = foot from origin to line AP. So M_AE = A - (A·v)/|v|^2 v. Then E = 2 M_AE - A = A - 2 (A·v)/|v|^2 v. So consistent.

Similarly, for Γ with center N=(c,0), the midpoint of AF is the foot of perpendicular from N to line AP. So M_AF = A - ((A-c)·v)/|v|^2 v? Actually, foot from N to line: point on line A + t v such that (A + t v - N) · v = 0 => (A-N)·v + t |v|^2 = 0 => t = - (A-N)·v/|v|^2. So M_AF = A - ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2) v. Then F = 2 M_AF - A = A - 2 ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2 v.

So we have expressions for E and F.

Now we want circle through B=(a,-h), E, F. Since E and F are symmetric in some sense? Not symmetric about x-axis because A and B are symmetric. But note that if we reflect the whole configuration across the x-axis, A becomes B, and the line AP becomes a line through B and the reflection of P across x-axis. That reflected line is not AP generally. However, maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is symmetric with respect to the line through H parallel to AP? Not.

Maybe we can compute the equation of circle (BEF) using the fact that it is the circle through B and the two points on line AP. Let's denote the line AP: l. The points E and F are on l. So the circle through B, E, F is the circle through B and the chord EF on l. There is a known formula: The power of a point X on l with respect to the circle is (X-B) times something? Actually, if we consider the circle through B and the two points E and F on l, then for any point X on l, the power of X w.r.t. the circle is (X-E)(X-F) (signed distances along l) if we take the line as a coordinate? But careful: The power of a point on the line l is the product of distances from that point to the two intersection points of the line with the circle. Since the line l intersects the circle at E and F, for any point X on l, the power is (XE)*(XF) (with sign if directed). So for point H? H is not on l generally. But if we want to show that line L through H parallel to l is tangent to the circle, we could consider the pole-polar relationship: The polar of a point on l is the line through the other intersection? Not.

Alternatively, we can use the concept of the radical axis: The line L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the power of H with respect to that circle equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but that's not.

Maybe we can compute the condition that the line through H parallel to l is tangent to the circle by showing that the distance from the circumcenter O to the line equals the radius. That can be expressed as: the power of H with respect to the circle is equal to the square of the distance from H to the point where the line from H perpendicular to l meets the circle? Not.

Let's compute O and radius explicitly. Let's denote coordinates in terms of a,h,c,r,R. We have p = (c+R-r)/2. Also, we have y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). Let’s also compute v = (p-a, y_P - h). For simplicity, let’s compute using the relations. We might also find that t_E and t_F have simple expressions in terms of p and a and c. Let's compute A·v and (A-c)·v.

We have v = (p-a, y_P - h). We can compute A·v = a(p-a) + h(y_P - h) = a(p-a) + h y_P - h^2. But y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). So h y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/2. Also, h^2 = r^2 - a^2. So A·v = a(p-a) - (a+r)(c+R-r)/2 - (r^2 - a^2). Similarly, (A-c)·v = (a-c)(p-a) + h(y_P - h) = (a-c)(p-a) + h y_P - h^2 = (a-c)(p-a) - (a+r)(c+R-r)/2 - (r^2 - a^2). So these are messy.

But perhaps we can find that t_E = (p-a)/something? Alternatively, we can compute E and F directly by solving the line and circle equations. Since we have line AP: we can write its equation in terms of A and P. Let’s find the line's equation in terms of x and y. We have A(a,h) and P(p, y_P). The line can be written as: (y - h)/(x - a) = (y_P - h)/(p - a) = slope m_l. So we can denote m = (y_P - h)/(p - a). Then line: y = h + m (x - a). So y = m x + (h - m a). Now we need intersection with circle x^2+y^2=r^2. Substitute: x^2 + (m x + (h - m a))^2 = r^2. Expand: x^2 + m^2 x^2 + 2m(h - m a)x + (h - m a)^2 = r^2 => (1+m^2)x^2 + 2m(h - m a)x + (h - m a)^2 - r^2 = 0. Since A is on both, x=a is a root. So the quadratic factors as (x - a)( (1+m^2)x + something ) = 0. Let's find the other root x_E. The sum of roots: a + x_E = - [2m(h - m a)]/(1+m^2). So x_E = - [2m(h - m a)]/(1+m^2) - a. Then y_E = m x_E + (h - m a). That gives E coordinates. Similarly, for Γ: (x-c)^2 + y^2 = R^2. Substitute y = m x + (h - m a). Then (x-c)^2 + (m x + (h - m a))^2 = R^2. Expand: (1+m^2)x^2 + 2(m(h - m a) - c? Actually compute: (x-c)^2 = x^2 - 2cx + c^2. So sum: x^2 - 2cx + c^2 + m^2 x^2 + 2m(h - m a)x + (h - m a)^2 = (1+m^2)x^2 + (2m(h - m a) - 2c)x + (c^2 + (h - m a)^2) = R^2. So equation: (1+m^2)x^2 + 2(m(h - m a) - c)x + (c^2 + (h - m a)^2 - R^2) = 0. Since A is on Γ, x=a is a root. So the other root x_F =? Sum of roots: a + x_F = -2(m(h - m a) - c)/(1+m^2). So x_F = -2(m(h - m a) - c)/(1+m^2) - a. Then y_F = m x_F + (h - m a). So we have explicit coordinates for E and F in terms of a, h, m, c. But m is slope of AP. We can also compute m in terms of a,h,c,r,R. Since we have P = (p, y_P) with p = (c+R-r)/2, y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). Then m = (y_P - h)/(p - a). That might simplify using the relations.

Let's compute m. We have p - a = (c+R-r)/2 - a. And y_P - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h. Combine over 2h: = -( (a+r)(c+R-r) + 2h^2 )/(2h). But 2h^2 = 2(r^2 - a^2) = 2(r-a)(r+a). So numerator = (a+r)(c+R-r) + 2(r+a)(r-a) = (a+r)[c+R-r + 2(r-a)] = (a+r)[c+R+r-2a]. So y_P - h = -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h). So m = [ -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h) ] / [ (c+R-r)/2 - a ] = [ -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h) ] / [ (c+R-r - 2a)/2 ] = [ -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h) ] * [2/(c+R-r-2a)] = -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a) / [ h (c+R-r-2a) ].

So m = - (a+r)(c+R+r-2a) / [ h (c+R-r-2a) ].

Now note that c+R-r-2a = (c+R-r) - 2a = 2p - 2a = 2(p-a). So denominator = h * 2(p-a). So m = - (a+r)(c+R+r-2a) / (2h(p-a)). But we also have c+R+r-2a =? Let's compute: c+R+r-2a = (c+R-r-2a) + 2r? Not. Actually, we can express c+R+r-2a = (c+R-r-2a) + 2r = 2(p-a) + 2r = 2(p-a+r). So m = - (a+r) * 2(p-a+r) / (2h(p-a)) = - (a+r)(p-a+r) / [ h (p-a) ].

Thus m = - (a+r)(p-a+r) / [ h (p-a) ].

Now we have m expressed in terms of a, h, p, r. But we also have relation between a, h, r, p? Since p = (c+R-r)/2, and a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c). Not simple.

Maybe we can find a relation between p-a and something. Note that p-a = (c+R-r)/2 - a. And we also have a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c). Now also compute p-a+r =? p-a+r = (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c) + r = ( (R-r)(c+R+r) + 2cr )/(2c) = ( (R-r)(c+R+r) + 2cr )/(2c). Expand (R-r)(c+R+r) = (R-r)c + (R-r)(R+r) = c(R-r) + (R^2 - r^2). Now also compute a+r? a+r =? We have a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c), so a+r = (c^2+r^2-R^2+2cr)/(2c) = ((c+r)^2 - R^2)/(2c) = (c+r-R)(c+r+R)/(2c). So a+r = ((c+r-R)(c+r+R))/(2c).

Now we have m = - (a+r)(p-a+r) / [ h (p-a) ] = - [ ((c+r-R)(c+r+R))/(2c) ] * [ p (R+r)/c ] / [ h * p (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c) ]? Actually, p-a = (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c). So h(p-a) = h (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c). And (a+r)(p-a+r) = ((c+r-R)(c+r+R)/(2c)) * (p (R+r)/c) = p (R+r)(c+r-R)(c+r+R)/(2c^2). Simplify: (c+r+R) = (c+R+r). So numerator: p (R+r)(c+r-R)(c+R+r). Denominator: c h (R-r)(c+R+r). Cancel (c+R+r): m = - p (R+r)(c+r-R) / [ c h (R-r) ].

Now note that c+r-R =? Since R>r, c+r-R could be positive or negative depending on c. In our numeric, c=1, r=5, R=5.83, so c+r-R=1+5-5.83=0.17, positive. So m = - p (R+r)(c+r-R) / [ c h (R-r) ].

Now we also have p = (c+R-r)/2. So m = - (c+R-r)/2 * (R+r)(c+r-R) / [ c h (R-r) ] = - (c+R-r)(R+r)(c+r-R) / [ 2 c h (R-r) ].

This is a nice expression for the slope m. Now we also have h^2 = r^2 - a^2. But we can also find h in terms of c,r,R? Using the formula: h^2 = r^2 - ((c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c))^2. That is standard. Now we have expressions for E and F in terms of a, h, m, c. But we also have a and h expressed in terms of c,r,R. Perhaps we can compute the circumcircle of BEF in a nicer way. Since we have explicit coordinates for E and F, we can compute the circle equation. Let's do that.

We have line l: y = m x + b, where b = h - m a. So l: y = m x + (h - m a). Also, we have B = (a, -h). Now E and F are points on l with x-coordinates x_E and x_F as derived. We can find the equation of circle through B, E, F. Since E and F lie on l, the circle's equation can be expressed as: (x^2+y^2) + u x + v y + w = 0. But perhaps we can find that the center O is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of BE and the perpendicular bisector of BF. Alternatively, we can use the fact that for a chord on l, the circle is determined by the condition that the power of a point on l is product of distances. For point H (not on l), the condition that line through H parallel to l is tangent to the circle can be expressed in terms of the polar. In fact, if we take the line through H parallel to l, then the condition that this line is tangent to the circle is equivalent to the condition that the pole of H with respect to the circle lies on l? Not exactly.

Let's think about the geometry: We have a circle (BEF) and a line L through H parallel to l. L is tangent to the circle if and only if the line through H perpendicular to l meets the circle at a point that is the point of tangency, and the distance from H to that point is the tangent length. That is not simple.

Given the difficulty, I think an analytic solution is the most straightforward to ensure rigor. We have derived expressions for x_E, x_F, and also for y_E, y_F. We can compute the circumcircle of BEF using the fact that points E and F lie on line l: y = αx + h. And B is (0,-h). We can find the equation of the circle through B and the two points on l. There is a known formula: If we have a line l and a point B not on l, then the family of circles through B and intersecting l at two points can be expressed as: (x^2+y^2) + Ux + Vy + W = 0 with the condition that the line l satisfies something. x_E = -2α b/(1+α^2) - a.
x_F = 2(d - α b)/(1+α^2) - a.

Now, note that b = h - α a. So α b = α h - α^2 a. Then 2α b/(1+α^2) = 2(α h - α^2 a)/(1+α^2) = 2α h/(1+α^2) - 2α^2 a/(1+α^2). So x_E = - [2α h/(1+α^2) - 2α^2 a/(1+α^2)] - a = -2α h/(1+α^2) + 2α^2 a/(1+α^2) - a = -2α h/(1+α^2) + a(2α^2/(1+α^2) - 1) = -2α h/(1+α^2) + a( (2α^2 - (1+α^2))/(1+α^2) ) = -2α h/(1+α^2) + a( (α^2 - 1)/(1+α^2) ) = -2α h/(1+α^2) - a(1-α^2)/(1+α^2). So x_E = - [2α h + a(1-α^2)]/(1+α^2). But note that a(1-α^2) = a - aα^2. So x_E = - (a + 2α h - aα^2)/(1+α^2). This is not symmetric.

Similarly, x_F = 2(d - α b)/(1+α^2) - a = 2(d - α(h - α a))/(1+α^2) - a = 2(d - α h + α^2 a)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2α h + 2α^2 a)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2α h + 2α^2 a - a(1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2α h + 2α^2 a - a - aα^2)/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2α h + α^2 a - a)/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2α h - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). So x_F = (2d - 2α h - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2).

Now, we also have y_E = α x_E + b = α x_E + h - α a = α(x_E - a) + h. But x_E - a = -2α b/(1+α^2) - 2a? Actually, from x_E = -2α b/(1+α^2) - a, so x_E - a = -2α b/(1+α^2) - 2a. Then y_E = -2α^2 b/(1+α^2) - 2α a + α a + b = -2α^2 b/(1+α^2) - α a + b = b - α a - 2α^2 b/(1+α^2) = (b(1 - 2α^2/(1+α^2))) - α a = b( (1+α^2 - 2α^2)/(1+α^2) ) - α a = b( (1-α^2)/(1+α^2) ) - α a. But b = h - α a, so y_E = (h - α a)(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a[ (1-α^2)/(1+α^2) + 1 ] = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a[ (1-α^2 + 1+α^2)/(1+α^2) ] = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a[ 2/(1+α^2) ] = [ h(1-α^2) - 2α a ]/(1+α^2). Given the time, I'll try to compute using the determinant formula for the circumcenter of BEF. We have expressions for x_E, y_E, x_F, y_F in terms of a, h, α, b, d. But we also have relations that simplify these expressions. Let's try to find simpler expressions for x_E and x_F using the fact that E and F are the second intersections of line l with circles Ω and Γ. There is a known formula: For a line through A with slope α, the x-coordinate of the other intersection with circle centered at origin is given by: x_E = - (a(1-α^2) + 2αh)/(1+α^2)? Actually, from the quadratic: (1+α^2)x^2 + 2αb x + (b^2 - r^2)=0. Since x=a is a root, we have (1+α^2)a^2 + 2αb a + b^2 - r^2 = 0. So the other root x_E satisfies: a + x_E = -2αb/(1+α^2). So x_E = -2αb/(1+α^2) - a. So we need to compute -2αb/(1+α^2). But we also have b = h - αa. So -2αb = -2α(h - αa) = -2αh + 2α^2a. So x_E = (-2αh + 2α^2a)/(1+α^2) - a = -2αh/(1+α^2) + a(2α^2/(1+α^2) - 1) = -2αh/(1+α^2) + a((2α^2 - (1+α^2))/(1+α^2)) = -2αh/(1+α^2) + a(α^2-1)/(1+α^2) = - (2αh + a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). So x_E = - (2αh + a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). Similarly, x_F = 2(d - αb)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2αb)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2α(h - αa))/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2αh + 2α^2a)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2αh + 2α^2a - a(1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2αh - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). So x_F = (2d - 2αh - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2).

Now, we also have y_E = α x_E + b = α x_E + h - αa = α(x_E - a) + h. And x_E - a = - (2αh + a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2) - a = - (2αh + a(1-α^2) + a(1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = - (2αh + a(1-α^2+1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = - (2αh + 2a)/(1+α^2) = -2(αh + a)/(1+α^2). So y_E = α * (-2(αh+a)/(1+α^2)) + h = -2α^2h/(1+α^2) -2αa/(1+α^2) + h = h(1 - 2α^2/(1+α^2)) - 2αa/(1+α^2) = h((1+α^2-2α^2)/(1+α^2)) - 2αa/(1+α^2) = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - 2αa/(1+α^2) = [h(1-α^2) - 2αa]/(1+α^2). So y_E = (h(1-α^2) - 2αa)/(1+α^2). Good.

Similarly, y_F = α x_F + b = α * (2d - 2αh - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2) + h - αa = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa(1-α^2)]/(1+α^2) + h - αa = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a]/(1+α^2) + h - αa. Write h - αa = (h(1+α^2) - αa(1+α^2))/(1+α^2). So y_F = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a + h(1+α^2) - αa(1+α^2)]/(1+α^2) = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a + h + hα^2 - αa - α^3a]/(1+α^2) = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a + h + hα^2 - αa - α^3a]/(1+α^2) = [2αd + h + hα^2 - 2α^2h - 2αa]/(1+α^2) = [2αd + h(1+α^2-2α^2) - 2αa]/(1+α^2) = [2αd + h(1-α^2) - 2αa]/(1+α^2). So y_F = (2αd + h(1-α^2) - 2αa)/(1+α^2). This is consistent with earlier.

Given the time, I'll attempt a computational proof with the help of some algebraic simplifications. Let's set up the problem in a coordinate system that simplifies the expressions for E and F. - P = (p, 0) with p > 0 (since AP is a radius, P is the center of ω, and A is on ω, so the other intersection of AP with ω is A' = (2p, 0) because P is the midpoint of AA'? So A is at (0,0), and the other intersection with x-axis is (2p,0). So A'=(2p,0). Good.

- The line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. Since A is at origin, let B = (b_x, b_y). Then the midpoint of AB is (b_x/2, b_y/2). The line MN is perpendicular to AB, so its direction is perpendicular to vector (b_x, b_y). So its equation: (x - b_x/2, y - b_y/2) · (b_x, b_y) = 0 => b_x(x - b_x/2) + b_y(y - b_y/2) = 0 => b_x x + b_y y = (b_x^2+b_y^2)/2 = |B|^2/2. So MN: b_x x + b_y y = |B|^2/2. Since B lies on both circles, we have that M and N are points on MN such that the circles pass through A and B. Also, M and N are the centers of Ω and Γ. Their distances to A are r and R, so MA = r, NA = R. Also, M and N are on MN. So M and N are the points on MN such that the distance from M to A is r, and from M to B is also r. So M is the intersection of MN with the perpendicular bisector of AB? Actually, the perpendicular bisector of AB is MN itself, so M lies on that line. So M is the point on MN that is at distance r from A. So M is the point on MN such that MA = r. Similarly, N is the point on MN such that NA = R. So M and N are the points on MN that satisfy that the distances to A are given. Since MN is a line, we can param M and N as points on that line. Let's denote the line MN: L: b_x x + b_y y = |B|^2/2. Let’s also denote the distance from A to any point X on L. The set of points X on L such that XA = r is the intersection of L with the circle centered at A radius r. That is a circle with center A radius r. So M is one of the intersection points of L with that circle. Similarly, N is one of the intersection points of L with the circle centered at A radius R. So M and N are determined by the geometry of A and the line L.

Now, we also have points C and D on L such that C, M, N, D are collinear in that order. And C is on Ω (circle centered at M through A), D is on Γ (circle centered at N through A). So C and D are the points on L such that the distances to M and N are r and R respectively, and they are on the same line L. But note that A is also on both circles, but A is not on L generally. So C is the second intersection of L with circle Ω. Similarly, D is the second intersection of L with circle Γ.

Thus, we have a configuration: Line L (MN) is the perpendicular bisector of AB. On L, we have points M and N (centers) and also points C and D (on circles). And we have the circle ω (center P) passing through A, C, D. So ω is the circle with diameter? Not.

Since ω is a circle through A, and L is some line, the chord CD of ω on L has its midpoint at the foot of the perpendicular from P to L. That foot is Q, the projection of P onto L. So Q is the midpoint of CD. Also, since A is on ω, and A is not on L generally, the line L will intersect ω at two points: C and D. So C and D are the two intersections. Now, we also have that M and N are points on L such that MA = r and NA = R, and also M is the center of Ω that passes through A and C, and N is the center of Γ that passes through A and D. So if we find the foot Q from A to L, then B = 2Q. The foot Q is given by: Q = ( -m l/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) )? Actually, the foot of perpendicular from origin to line y = lx + m is: ( -m l/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) ). Because the line's equation: lx - y + m = 0. The foot from origin is ( -A C/(A^2+B^2) )? Standard formula: For line ax+by+c=0, foot from (x0,y0) is (x0 - a*(ax0+by0+c)/(a^2+b^2), y0 - b*(ax0+by0+c)/(a^2+b^2)). Here, a=l, b=-1, c=m. So ax0+by0+c = 0 + 0 + m = m. So foot Q = ( -l*m/(l^2+1), -(-1)*m/(l^2+1) ) = ( -l m/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) ). So Q = ( -l m/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) ). Then B = 2Q = ( -2l m/(1+l^2), 2m/(1+l^2) ). So B is determined by l and m.

Now, C and D are the intersections of L with ω. Substitute y = lx + m into ω: x^2 + (lx+m)^2 = 2px => x^2 + l^2 x^2 + 2l m x + m^2 = 2px => (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(l m - p)x + m^2 = 0. This quadratic gives the x-coordinates of C and D. Let the roots be x_C and x_D. Then the product x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2). The sum x_C + x_D = -2(l m - p)/(1+l^2) = 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2). So we have x_C and x_D.

Now, we also have that M is the circumcenter of A and C. Since A=0, the circumcenter of A and C is the midpoint of the perpendicular bisector of AC. But we can also find M as the point on L such that MA = MC. Since A is origin, MA = |M|. And MC = |M - C|. So condition: |M| = |M - C|. Squaring: |M|^2 = |M|^2 - 2 M·C + |C|^2 => 2 M·C = |C|^2 => M·C = |C|^2/2. So M is the point on L such that the dot product with C equals half of |C|^2. Since M lies on L, we can param M as (t, l t + m). Then M·C = t x_C + (l t + m) y_C = t x_C + l t y_C + m y_C = t (x_C + l y_C) + m y_C. But note that y_C = l x_C + m, so x_C + l y_C = x_C + l(l x_C + m) = x_C + l^2 x_C + l m = (1+l^2)x_C + l m. So M·C = t[(1+l^2)x_C + l m] + m y_C. And |C|^2 = x_C^2 + y_C^2. So we have t[(1+l^2)x_C + l m] + m y_C = (x_C^2 + y_C^2)/2. This gives t. So M is determined by C.

Similarly, N is the point on L such that N·D = |D|^2/2.

Now, we also have that E = (2M_x, 0) and F = (2N_x, 0). So E and F are points on the x-axis.

Given the time, I think we can try to compute H explicitly using the formula for orthocenter in terms of coordinates. Let's denote:

P = (p,0)
M = (M_x, M_y)
N = (N_x, N_y)

We can compute the orthocenter H as:

H = ( x_H, y_H ) such that:

(1) (H - P) · (M - N) = 0 (since altitude from P is perpendicular to MN)
(2) (H - M) · (P - N) = 0 (altitude from M is perpendicular to PN)

So we have:

(H_x - p, H_y) · (M_x - N_x, M_y - N_y) = 0  => (H_x - p)(M_x - N_x) + H_y (M_y - N_y) = 0
(H_x - M_x, H_y - M_y) · (P_x - N_x, P_y - N_y) = 0 => (H_x - M_x)(p - N_x) + (H_y - M_y)(-N_y) = 0

We can solve for H_x, H_y. This is a linear system. We can write:

(1) (M_x - N_x) H_x + (M_y - N_y) H_y = p (M_x - N_x)
(2) (p - N_x) H_x - N_y H_y = M_x (p - N_x) + M_y N_y

Actually, from second: (H_x - M_x)(p - N_x) + (H_y - M_y)(-N_y)=0 => (p - N_x) H_x - N_y H_y = M_x (p - N_x) + M_y N_y.

So we have:

A H_x + B H_y = C
D H_x + E H_y = F

where A = M_x - N_x, B = M_y - N_y, C = p (M_x - N_x)
D = p - N_x, E = -N_y, F = M_x (p - N_x) + M_y N_y.

Then we can solve for H_y. The solution will involve M and N.

Now, we also have that the horizontal line through H is y = H_y. We need to show that this line is tangent to circle through B, E, F. That circle's equation can be found using coordinates. Since E and F are on x-axis, we can find the circle's equation by using the fact that the circle passes through B, (2M_x,0), (2N_x,0). So we can write the circle's equation as: x^2+y^2 + Ux + Vy + W=0. Since it passes through (2M_x,0): (2M_x)^2 + U(2M_x) + W = 0 => 4M_x^2 + 2U M_x + W = 0. Similarly, for (2N_x,0): 4N_x^2 + 2U N_x + W = 0. Subtracting: 4(M_x^2 - N_x^2) + 2U(M_x - N_x) = 0 => 2(M_x - N_x)(2(M_x+N_x) + U) = 0 => U = -2(M_x+N_x). Then from the equation with M_x: 4M_x^2 + 2(-2(M_x+N_x)) M_x + W = 4M_x^2 -4M_x(M_x+N_x)+W = 4M_x^2 -4M_x^2 -4M_x N_x + W = -4M_x N_x + W = 0 => W = 4M_x N_x.

Now, the circle also passes through B = (b_x, b_y). So b_x^2+b_y^2 + U b_x + V b_y + W = 0 => (b_x^2+b_y^2) -2(M_x+N_x) b_x + V b_y + 4M_x N_x = 0 => V b_y = - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x. Thus, V = [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y.

Now, the center O = (-U/2, -V/2) = (M_x+N_x, -V/2). The radius R_circ = sqrt( (U^2+V^2)/4 - W ) = sqrt( (4(M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2)/4 - 4M_x N_x ) = sqrt( (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x )? Actually, careful: U = -2(M_x+N_x), so U^2/4 = (4(M_x+N_x)^2)/4 = (M_x+N_x)^2. Then R^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x.

Now, we need to show that the horizontal line y = H_y is tangent to this circle. The distance from O to the line is |y_O - H_y| = | -V/2 - H_y | = | - (V/2 + H_y) | = | V/2 + H_y |. So we need to show that | V/2 + H_y | = R_circ. That is: (V/2 + H_y)^2 = R^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x.

Expand left: V^2/4 + V H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x => Cancel V^2/4: V H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4M_x N_x = (M_x^2+2M_x N_x+N_x^2) - 4M_x N_x = M_x^2 + N_x^2 - 2M_x N_x = (M_x - N_x)^2.

So we need to prove that V H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2. Or equivalently, H_y^2 + V H_y - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 0.

Given the difficulty, I'll attempt a computational proof with the help of some algebra. Since we have a clear coordinate setup, we can compute everything in terms of l, m, p. We have:

- L: y = l x + m.
- ω: x^2+y^2=2p x.
- C and D: x-coordinates satisfy (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(l m - p)x + m^2=0. Let x_C and x_D be roots.
- Then M_x = (p x_C - m y_C)/(x_C + l y_C) = (p x_C - m(l x_C+m))/(x_C + l(l x_C+m)) = ((p - l m)x_C - m^2)/((1+l^2)x_C + l m). Similarly, N_x = ((p - l m)x_D - m^2)/((1+l^2)x_D + l m).
- Also, M_y = l M_x + m, N_y = l N_x + m.
- B = ( -2l m/(1+l^2), 2m/(1+l^2) ) = 2Q, where Q = (-l m/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2)).
- E = (2M_x, 0), F = (2N_x, 0).

Now, we need H, orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since we have coordinates, we can compute H using the formula for orthocenter in terms of coordinates. Let's denote:

P = (p,0)
M = (M_x, M_y)
N = (N_x, N_y)

We can compute the orthocenter H as:

H_x = ( (M_x N_y - M_y N_x) + p (M_y - N_y) ) / (M_x - N_x) ??? Actually, there is a formula using determinants. Let's derive from the altitude equations:

Altitude from P: (x - p, y) · (M - N) = 0 => (x - p)(M_x - N_x) + y (M_y - N_y) = 0.
Altitude from M: (x - M_x, y - M_y) · (P - N) = 0 => (x - M_x)(p - N_x) + (y - M_y)(-N_y) = 0 => (p - N_x)x - N_y y = M_x(p - N_x) + M_y N_y.

So we have:

A1 x + B1 y = C1, with A1 = M_x - N_x, B1 = M_y - N_y, C1 = p (M_x - N_x).
A2 x + B2 y = C2, with A2 = p - N_x, B2 = -N_y, C2 = M_x(p - N_x) + M_y N_y.

Then we can solve for y. The solution:

y = (C1 A2 - C2 A1) / (B1 A2 - B2 A1). So we can compute H_y.

Thus, H_y = ( p (M_x - N_x)(p - N_x) - [M_x(p - N_x) + M_y N_y] (M_x - N_x) ) / ( (M_y - N_y)(p - N_x) - (-N_y)(M_x - N_x) ) = (M_x - N_x)[ p(p - N_x) - M_x(p - N_x) - M_y N_y ] / ( (M_y - N_y)(p - N_x) + N_y(M_x - N_x) ) = (M_x - N_x)[ (p - M_x)(p - N_x) - M_y N_y ] / ( (M_y - N_y)(p - N_x) + N_y(M_x - N_x) ).

So H_y = (M_x - N_x) * ( (p - M_x)(p - N_x) - M_y N_y ) / ( (M_y - N_y)(p - N_x) + N_y(M_x - N_x) ).

Now, note that M_y = l M_x + m, N_y = l N_x + m. So M_y - N_y = l (M_x - N_x). Also, M_y N_y = (l M_x + m)(l N_x + m) = l^2 M_x N_x + l m (M_x + N_x) + m^2.

Also, (p - M_x)(p - N_x) = p^2 - p(M_x+N_x) + M_x N_x.

So numerator factor: (p - M_x)(p - N_x) - M_y N_y = p^2 - p(M_x+N_x) + M_x N_x - l^2 M_x N_x - l m (M_x+N_x) - m^2 = p^2 - (p + l m)(M_x+N_x) + (1 - l^2) M_x N_x - m^2.

Denominator: (M_y - N_y)(p - N_x) + N_y(M_x - N_x) = l (M_x - N_x)(p - N_x) + (l N_x + m)(M_x - N_x) = (M_x - N_x)[ l(p - N_x) + l N_x + m ] = (M_x - N_x)[ l p - l N_x + l N_x + m ] = (M_x - N_x)( l p + m ).

Thus, H_y = (M_x - N_x) * [ p^2 - (p + l m)(M_x+N_x) + (1 - l^2) M_x N_x - m^2 ] / [ (M_x - N_x)( l p + m ) ] = [ p^2 - (p + l m)(M_x+N_x) + (1 - l^2) M_x N_x - m^2 ] / ( l p + m ).

So H_y = ( p^2 - (p + l m)(M_x+N_x) + (1 - l^2) M_x N_x - m^2 ) / ( l p + m ).

That's a nice simplification! Good.

Now, we need to compute V and then check that H_y^2 + V H_y = (M_x - N_x)^2.

Recall V = [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y.

We have B = (b_x, b_y) = ( -2l m/(1+l^2), 2m/(1+l^2) ). So b_x^2+b_y^2 = (4l^2 m^2 + 4m^2)/(1+l^2)^2 = 4m^2(l^2+1)/(1+l^2)^2 = 4m^2/(1+l^2). So b_x^2+b_y^2 = 4m^2/(1+l^2). Also, b_y = 2m/(1+l^2). So - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x = -4m^2/(1+l^2) + 2(M_x+N_x)(-2l m/(1+l^2)) - 4M_x N_x = -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x.

Thus, V = [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] / (2m/(1+l^2)) = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ].

Simplify inside: = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) ] - (1+l^2)/(2m) * 4M_x N_x = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) ] - 2(1+l^2) M_x N_x / m.

But (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) ] = -2m, and (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) ] = -2l (M_x+N_x). So V = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - 2(1+l^2) M_x N_x / m.

Thus, V = -2[ m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2) M_x N_x / m ].

Now, we need to compute H_y^2 + V H_y = (M_x - N_x)^2. Since H_y is given by expression, we can plug in and simplify using the relations between M_x,N_x and l,m,p. Also, note that M_x and N_x are the x-coordinates of the circumcenters of A-C and A-D, which are related to the roots of the quadratic. Perhaps we can find expressions for M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of l,m,p.

Let's compute M_x and N_x using the expressions we have. We have:

M_x = ((p - l m)x_C - m^2) / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)
N_x = ((p - l m)x_D - m^2) / ((1+l^2)x_D + l m)

where x_C and x_D are roots of (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(l m - p)x + m^2=0.

Let’s denote A = 1+l^2, B = 2(l m - p), C = m^2. Then the quadratic is A x^2 + B x + C = 0. So x_C + x_D = -B/A = 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2), x_C x_D = C/A = m^2/(1+l^2).

Now, define for each root x, the denominator d_x = A x + l m (since (1+l^2)x + l m). Then M_x = ( (p - l m) x - m^2 ) / d_x. Similarly, N_x = ( (p - l m) x_D - m^2 ) / d_D.

We can also write M_x = (p - l m) x - m^2 divided by (A x + l m). So M_x = (p - l m) x - m^2 / (A x + l m). This is reminiscent of the transformation from x to M_x. Perhaps we can find that M_x and N_x are the two solutions of some quadratic equation in terms of l,m,p. Let's try to find a relation between M_x and the root x. We have M_x = (p - l m) x - m^2 / (A x + l m). Cross-multiply: M_x (A x + l m) = (p - l m) x - m^2 => A M_x x + l m M_x = (p - l m) x - m^2 => (A M_x - (p - l m)) x = - l m M_x - m^2 => x = - (l m M_x + m^2) / (A M_x - (p - l m)). So x is expressed in terms of M_x. Since x is a root of A x^2 + B x + C = 0, we can substitute this expression to get an equation for M_x. That will be a quadratic equation whose two roots are M_x and N_x. So we can find M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of p,l,m.

Let's do that. We have:

x = - (l m M_x + m^2) / (A M_x - (p - l m)) = - m (l M_x + m) / (A M_x - (p - l m)).

Let’s denote D = A M_x - (p - l m). Then x = - m (l M_x + m) / D.

Now, plug into A x^2 + B x + C = 0. Compute:

A * [ m^2 (l M_x + m)^2 / D^2 ] + B * [ - m (l M_x + m) / D ] + C = 0.

Multiply by D^2:

A m^2 (l M_x + m)^2 - B m (l M_x + m) D + C D^2 = 0.

But D = A M_x - (p - l m). So we have:

A m^2 (l M_x + m)^2 - B m (l M_x + m) (A M_x - (p - l m)) + C (A M_x - (p - l m))^2 = 0.

This is a quadratic equation in M_x. Since the roots are M_x and N_x, we can compute the sum and product of roots. But note that this equation might be linear in M_x? Let's expand and see.

First, recall A = 1+l^2, B = 2(l m - p), C = m^2.

Also, note that (p - l m) =? That's fine.

We can try to compute the sum and product of M_x and N_x by using Vieta on this quadratic. Let's expand the expression in terms of M_x.

Let’s denote M = M_x for simplicity.

Expression: A m^2 (l M + m)^2 - B m (l M + m) (A M - (p - l m)) + C (A M - (p - l m))^2 = 0.

Expand each term:

First term: A m^2 (l^2 M^2 + 2 l m M + m^2) = A m^2 l^2 M^2 + 2 A l m^3 M + A m^4.

Second term: - B m (l M + m) (A M - (p - l m)) = -B m [ (l M)(A M - (p - l m)) + m (A M - (p - l m)) ] = -B m [ l A M^2 - l(p - l m) M + A m M - m(p - l m) ] = -B m [ l A M^2 + ( - l(p - l m) + A m ) M - m(p - l m) ].

So second term = -B m l A M^2 - B m ( - l(p - l m) + A m ) M + B m * m(p - l m) = -B l A m M^2 - B m ( - l(p - l m) + A m ) M + B m^2 (p - l m).

Third term: C (A M - (p - l m))^2 = C (A^2 M^2 - 2 A (p - l m) M + (p - l m)^2) = C A^2 M^2 - 2 C A (p - l m) M + C (p - l m)^2.

Now, combine all terms:

Coefficient of M^2: from first: A m^2 l^2; from second: -B l A m; from third: C A^2. So coeff_M^2 = A m^2 l^2 - B l A m + C A^2 = A ( m^2 l^2 - B l m + C A ).

Coefficient of M: from first: 2 A l m^3; from second: -B m ( - l(p - l m) + A m ) = -B m ( - l(p - l m) + A m ) = -B m ( - l(p - l m) + A m ); from third: -2 C A (p - l m). So coeff_M = 2 A l m^3 - B m ( - l(p - l m) + A m ) - 2 C A (p - l m).

Constant term: from first: A m^4; from second: B m^2 (p - l m); from third: C (p - l m)^2. So const = A m^4 + B m^2 (p - l m) + C (p - l m)^2.

Now, note that this quadratic equation in M is satisfied by M_x and N_x. So we can compute M_x+N_x = - coeff_M / coeff_M^2, and M_x N_x = const / coeff_M^2.

But we might not need the explicit sum and product; we need to verify the relation H_y^2 + V H_y = (M_x - N_x)^2. Since H_y is expressed in terms of M_x+N_x and M_x N_x, and V is also expressed in terms of M_x+N_x and M_x N_x. So we can compute H_y^2 + V H_y and see if it equals (M_x - N_x)^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4 M_x N_x.

This is an algebraic identity that we need to verify using the relations between l,m,p and the coefficients. H_y = ( p^2 - (p + l m)(M_x+N_x) + (1 - l^2) M_x N_x - m^2 ) / ( l p + m ).

And V = -2[ m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2) M_x N_x / m ].

Given the time, I think we can attempt to compute H_y in terms of the parameters directly using the roots. Let's compute M_x and N_x explicitly using the roots. Perhaps we can find that M_x and N_x are given by simple expressions. Let's compute:

M_x = ((p - l m)x_C - m^2) / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)
N_x = ((p - l m)x_D - m^2) / ((1+l^2)x_D + l m)

We also have that x_C and x_D are roots of A x^2 + B x + C =0, where A=1+l^2, B=2(l m - p), C=m^2.

Let's define K = p - l m. Then M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/(A x_C + l m). Note that the denominator is A x_C + l m. Since x_C is a root, we have A x_C^2 + B x_C + C =0 => A x_C^2 + 2(l m - p)x_C + m^2=0 => A x_C^2 - 2K x_C + m^2=0. So A x_C^2 + m^2 = 2K x_C.

Now, we can try to simplify M_x. Let's compute M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/(A x_C + l m). Multiply numerator and denominator by something? Maybe we can express M_x in terms of x_C. Consider the function f(x) = (K x - m^2)/(A x + l m). We can compute f(x) =? Since A x + l m = (1+l^2)x + l m. Not obviously related.

We can also compute the reciprocal: 1/M_x = (A x_C + l m)/(K x_C - m^2). This might be simpler.

Alternatively, we can find that M_x and N_x are the two values of the transformation applied to the roots. Perhaps there is a known fact: For the circle x^2+y^2=2px and line y=lx+m, the intersection points C and D have coordinates that are symmetric with respect to the line through P perpendicular to L. The circumcenters of A-C and A-D lie on L. Actually, we have M and N on L. And we found that H_y = ( p^2 - (p+lm)(M_x+N_x) + (1-l^2)M_x N_x - m^2 )/(lp+m). This is reminiscent of the formula for the y-coordinate of the orthocenter of triangle with vertices at P, M, N. Given the time, I think we should try to compute M_x and N_x using the fact that they are the solutions of the quadratic we derived. That quadratic might simplify if we choose specific parameters. Since the problem is invariant under scaling, we can set p=1 by scaling the configuration? Because p is the x-coordinate of P, and we can scale the whole configuration by a factor. If we scale by a factor, then A=0 stays at origin, P becomes (p,0) scaled, but also the circle ω scales, and the line L scales. However, the condition we need to prove is homogeneous? Possibly we can set p=1. But careful: In the scaling, the circle ω: x^2+y^2=2p x. If we scale all coordinates by a factor k, then the new circle becomes (kx)^2+(ky)^2 = 2p (kx) => k^2(x^2+y^2)=2p k x => x^2+y^2 = (2p/k) x. So the new p' = p/k. So scaling changes p. So we can set p=1 by scaling appropriately. However, we also have that the line L is defined by l and m, which will also scale. So we can set p=1 without loss of generality because we can scale the entire configuration (including the circles) by a factor that makes p=1. Since the problem involves tangency, scaling preserves tangency. So we can set p=1.

Thus, let p=1. Then ω: x^2+y^2=2x.

Now, L: y = l x + m.

Then the quadratic: (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(l m - 1)x + m^2=0.

So x_C + x_D = 2(1 - l m)/(1+l^2), x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2).

Now, M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/(A x_C + l m), where K = p - l m = 1 - l m, A=1+l^2.

Thus, M_x = ((1 - l m)x_C - m^2)/((1+l^2)x_C + l m). Similarly, N_x = ((1 - l m)x_D - m^2)/((1+l^2)x_D + l m).

Now, we need to compute H_y and V.

H_y = ( p^2 - (p+lm)(M_x+N_x) + (1-l^2)M_x N_x - m^2 )/( l p + m ) = ( 1 - (1+lm)(M_x+N_x) + (1-l^2)M_x N_x - m^2 )/( l + m ). Since p=1.

So H_y = ( 1 - (1+lm)(M_x+N_x) + (1-l^2)M_x N_x - m^2 )/( l + m ).

V = -2[ m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2)M_x N_x / m ].

Now, we need to verify that H_y^2 + V H_y = (M_x - N_x)^2.

This is a relation that must hold given the definitions. This is a purely algebraic identity that we can try to prove by substituting the expressions for M_x and N_x in terms of x_C and x_D. Since x_C and x_D are the two roots, we can use Vieta. Let's attempt to compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of l,m.

We have M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/(A x_C + l m). Let's define for a given root x, the denominator d(x)=A x + l m. Then M_x = (K x - m^2)/d(x). So we can write M_x = (K x - m^2)/d(x). Since x satisfies A x^2 - 2K x + m^2=0 (since B=2(lm-p)=2(lm-1)=-2K, so A x^2 - 2K x + m^2=0). So A x^2 + m^2 = 2K x.

Now, we can try to find an expression for M_x in terms of x. Consider the transformation T(x) = (K x - m^2)/(A x + l m). We can compute the sum and product of the two values T(x_C) and T(x_D) by using the fact that x_C and x_D are roots. There is a formula for the sum and product of T(x) when x runs over the roots of a quadratic. This is a standard technique: If x satisfies ax^2+bx+c=0, then for T(x)= (α x + β)/(γ x + δ), the sum and product of T(x_i) can be expressed in terms of the coefficients. In fact, we can find that T(x) satisfies a quadratic equation: (γ^2 a - δ^2 a?) Actually, we can derive the quadratic equation for T. Let y = T(x). Then y = (α x + β)/(γ x + δ). Solve for x: y(γ x+δ)=α x+β => γ y x + δ y = α x+β => (γ y - α)x = β - δ y => x = (β - δ y)/(γ y - α). Substitute into ax^2+bx+c=0. This gives a quadratic in y. So the sum and product of y are given by Vieta. So we can compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x directly using the coefficients.

Let's do that: We have a = A = 1+l^2, b = -2K = -2(1-lm), c = m^2. And T(x) = (K x - m^2)/(A x + l m). So α = K, β = -m^2, γ = A, δ = l m. Then the quadratic for y is:

a ( (β - δ y)/(γ y - α) )^2 + b ( (β - δ y)/(γ y - α) ) + c = 0.

Multiply by (γ y - α)^2:

a (β - δ y)^2 + b (β - δ y)(γ y - α) + c (γ y - α)^2 = 0.

This is a quadratic in y. Let's expand:

a (β^2 - 2βδ y + δ^2 y^2) + b (βγ y - βα - δγ y^2 + δα y) + c (γ^2 y^2 - 2γα y + α^2) = 0.

Collect coefficients:

y^2: a δ^2 - b δγ + c γ^2.
y: -2aβδ + b(βγ + δα) - 2c γ α.
constant: a β^2 - b α β + c α^2.

Now, note that α = K = 1 - lm, β = -m^2, γ = A = 1+l^2, δ = l m.

We can compute these expressions. This will give us M_x+N_x and M_x N_x. Then we can compute H_y and V, and then verify the identity. This is a lot of algebra, but it's systematic. Let's compute the coefficients.

First, compute:

γ = 1+l^2.
δ = l m.
α = 1 - l m.
β = -m^2.

Now, compute:

a = γ = 1+l^2. (since a = A = γ)

We'll compute:

Coeff_y2 = a δ^2 - b δγ + c γ^2.

But b = -2K = -2α. Since α = 1 - lm, so b = -2α. And c = m^2.

Thus, Coeff_y2 = a δ^2 - (-2α) δγ + c γ^2 = a δ^2 + 2α δγ + c γ^2.

Now, δ = l m, γ = 1+l^2, α = 1 - l m.

So compute:

a δ^2 = (1+l^2) * (l^2 m^2) = l^2 m^2 (1+l^2).
2α δγ = 2 (1 - l m) * (l m) * (1+l^2) = 2 l m (1 - l m)(1+l^2).
c γ^2 = m^2 * (1+l^2)^2 = m^2 (1+l^2)^2.

Thus, Coeff_y2 = l^2 m^2 (1+l^2) + 2 l m (1 - l m)(1+l^2) + m^2 (1+l^2)^2 = (1+l^2) [ l^2 m^2 + 2 l m (1 - l m) + m^2 (1+l^2) ].

Simplify inside: l^2 m^2 + 2 l m - 2 l^2 m^2 + m^2 + l^2 m^2 = (l^2 m^2 -2l^2 m^2 + l^2 m^2) + 2 l m + m^2 = 0 + 2 l m + m^2 = m(2l + m). So Coeff_y2 = (1+l^2) * m (2l + m) = m (1+l^2)(2l+m).

Thus, Coeff_y2 = m (1+l^2)(2l+m).

Now, Coeff_y = -2aβδ + b(βγ + δα) - 2c γ α.

We have a = γ = 1+l^2, β = -m^2, δ = l m, b = -2α = -2(1-lm), c = m^2.

Compute term by term:

-2aβδ = -2 * (1+l^2) * (-m^2) * (l m) = 2 l m^3 (1+l^2).

b(βγ + δα) = -2α * (βγ + δα) = -2α * ( (-m^2)γ + (l m)α ) = -2α * ( -m^2 γ + l m α ) = -2α * m ( -m γ + l α ) = -2α m ( -m γ + l α ) = -2α m ( l α - m γ ).

-2c γ α = -2 * m^2 * γ * α = -2 m^2 γ α.

So Coeff_y = 2 l m^3 (1+l^2) -2α m ( l α - m γ ) -2 m^2 γ α.

Now, α = 1 - l m, γ = 1+l^2.

Compute l α - m γ = l(1 - l m) - m(1+l^2) = l - l^2 m - m - m l^2 = l - m - 2 l^2 m.

So -2α m (l α - m γ) = -2α m ( l - m - 2 l^2 m ). And -2 m^2 γ α = -2 m^2 γ α.

Thus, Coeff_y = 2 l m^3 (1+l^2) -2α m (l - m - 2 l^2 m) -2 m^2 γ α.

Now, α = 1 - l m. So let's expand everything in terms of l,m.

First, 2 l m^3 (1+l^2) = 2 l m^3 + 2 l^3 m^3.

Now, -2α m (l - m - 2 l^2 m) = -2 m (1 - l m)(l - m - 2 l^2 m). Expand (1 - l m)(l - m - 2 l^2 m) = (l - m - 2 l^2 m) - l m (l - m - 2 l^2 m) = l - m - 2 l^2 m - l^2 m + l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2 = l - m - 3 l^2 m + l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2. So then -2 m times that = -2 m [ l - m - 3 l^2 m + l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2 ] = -2 l m + 2 m^2 + 6 l^2 m^2 - 2 l m^3 - 4 l^3 m^3.

Now, -2 m^2 γ α = -2 m^2 (1+l^2)(1 - l m) = -2 m^2 (1 - l m + l^2 - l^3 m) = -2 m^2 + 2 l m^3 - 2 l^2 m^2 + 2 l^3 m^3.

Now, sum all:

From first: 2 l m^3 + 2 l^3 m^3.
From second: -2 l m + 2 m^2 + 6 l^2 m^2 - 2 l m^3 - 4 l^3 m^3.
From third: -2 m^2 + 2 l m^3 - 2 l^2 m^2 + 2 l^3 m^3.

Now, combine like terms:

Constant term? None.

l m term: -2 l m.
m^2 term: 2 m^2 - 2 m^2 = 0.
l^2 m^2 term: 6 l^2 m^2 - 2 l^2 m^2 = 4 l^2 m^2.
l m^3 term: 2 l m^3 - 2 l m^3 + 2 l m^3 = 2 l m^3.
l^3 m^3 term: 2 l^3 m^3 - 4 l^3 m^3 + 2 l^3 m^3 = 0.

So Coeff_y = -2 l m + 4 l^2 m^2 + 2 l m^3 = 2 l m ( -1 + 2 l m + m^2 ). So Coeff_y = 2 l m (2 l m + m^2 - 1).

Thus, Coeff_y = 2 l m (2 l m + m^2 - 1).

Now, constant term: Const = a β^2 - b α β + c α^2.

a = γ = 1+l^2, β = -m^2, so a β^2 = (1+l^2) m^4.
b = -2α, so -b α β = -(-2α) α β = 2α^2 β = 2α^2 (-m^2) = -2 m^2 α^2.
c = m^2, so c α^2 = m^2 α^2.
Thus, Const = (1+l^2) m^4 - 2 m^2 α^2 + m^2 α^2 = (1+l^2) m^4 - m^2 α^2 = m^2 [ (1+l^2) m^2 - α^2 ].

Now, α = 1 - l m, so α^2 = (1 - l m)^2 = 1 - 2 l m + l^2 m^2.
Thus, (1+l^2) m^2 - α^2 = m^2 + l^2 m^2 - (1 - 2 l m + l^2 m^2) = m^2 + l^2 m^2 -1 + 2 l m - l^2 m^2 = m^2 -1 + 2 l m.
So Const = m^2 (m^2 -1 + 2 l m) = m^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1).

Thus, constant term = m^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1).

Now, we have the quadratic in y: Coeff_y2 y^2 + Coeff_y y + Const = 0. So the sum and product of roots (M_x and N_x) are:

M_x+N_x = - Coeff_y / Coeff_y2 = - [2 l m (2 l m + m^2 - 1)] / [ m (1+l^2)(2l+m) ] = -2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / [ (1+l^2)(2l+m) ].

And M_x N_x = Const / Coeff_y2 = m^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / [ m (1+l^2)(2l+m) ] = m (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / [ (1+l^2)(2l+m) ].

Great! So we have explicit expressions for M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of l,m. Good.

Now, we also need H_y and V.

Recall:

H_y = ( 1 - (1+lm)(M_x+N_x) + (1-l^2)M_x N_x - m^2 ) / ( l + m ).

Plug in M_x+N_x and M_x N_x.

First, compute (1+lm)(M_x+N_x) = (1+lm) * [ -2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ].

And (1-l^2)M_x N_x = (1-l^2) * [ m (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ].

So H_y numerator = 1 - (1+lm) * [ -2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ] + (1-l^2) * [ m (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ] - m^2.

Simplify: = 1 - m^2 + [ (1+l^2)(2l+m) ]^{-1} [ (1+lm)*2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) + (1-l^2) m (2 l m + m^2 - 1) ]? Actually careful: The term is - (1+lm) * ( -2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ... ) = + (1+lm) * 2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ... So it's plus. So:

H_y numerator = 1 - m^2 + [ (1+lm)*2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) + (1-l^2) m (2 l m + m^2 - 1) ] / [ (1+l^2)(2l+m) ].

Factor (2 l m + m^2 - 1) in numerator: = (2 l m + m^2 - 1) [ 2 l (1+lm) + m (1-l^2) ].

So numerator = 1 - m^2 + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) * [ 2 l (1+lm) + m (1-l^2) ] / [ (1+l^2)(2l+m) ].

Now, denominator of H_y is (l+m). So H_y = [ 1 - m^2 + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) * Q / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ] / (l+m), where Q = 2 l (1+lm) + m (1-l^2).

Now, we need to compute V = -2[ m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2) M_x N_x / m ].

First, compute l(M_x+N_x) = l * [ -2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ] = -2 l^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ).

Next, (1+l^2) M_x N_x / m = (1+l^2) / m * [ m (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ] = (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / (2l+m).

So then m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2) M_x N_x / m = m - 2 l^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / (2l+m).

Combine the two terms with denominator (2l+m): = m + [ (2 l m + m^2 - 1) - 2 l^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / (1+l^2) ] / (2l+m). Factor (2 l m + m^2 - 1) in bracket: = m + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) * [ 1 - 2 l^2/(1+l^2) ] / (2l+m) = m + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) * [ (1+l^2 - 2 l^2)/(1+l^2) ] / (2l+m) = m + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) * [ (1 - l^2)/(1+l^2) ] / (2l+m).

Thus, V = -2[ m + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) * (1 - l^2)/( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) ].

So V = -2m - 2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1) * (1 - l^2) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ).

Now, we need to compute H_y^2 + V H_y. That is a rational expression in l,m. We can try to simplify by computing H_y explicitly. Perhaps H_y can be simplified to a nice form. Let's compute the numerator of H_y:

Let’s denote A = 1+l^2, B = 2l+m, C = 2 l m + m^2 - 1. Then:

H_y = [ 1 - m^2 + C * Q / (A B) ] / (l+m), where Q = 2 l (1+lm) + m (1-l^2). Compute Q.

Q = 2 l (1+lm) + m (1-l^2) = 2 l + 2 l^2 m + m - m l^2 = 2 l + m + l^2 m (2 - 1) = 2 l + m + l^2 m.

So Q = 2 l + m + l^2 m.

Now, compute C * Q = (2 l m + m^2 - 1)(2 l + m + l^2 m). Expand: Let's do it later.

Now, H_y numerator = 1 - m^2 + C Q/(A B). So H_y = (1 - m^2)/(l+m) + (C Q)/(A B (l+m)). So H_y = (1 - m^2)/(l+m) + (C Q)/(A B (l+m)).

Now, note that (1 - m^2)/(l+m) = (1-m)(1+m)/(l+m). Not necessarily simpler.

We also have V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B). So V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B).

Now, we need to compute H_y^2 + V H_y = (M_x - N_x)^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4 M_x N_x. We have M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of l,m. So we can compute (M_x - N_x)^2 directly. That might be simpler: (M_x - N_x)^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4 M_x N_x. Using our expressions:

M_x+N_x = -2 l C / (A B) (since C = 2 l m + m^2 - 1). Actually, M_x+N_x = -2 l C / (A B). Because M_x+N_x = -2 l (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) = -2 l C / (A B).

And M_x N_x = m C / (A B). Because M_x N_x = m (2 l m + m^2 - 1) / ( (1+l^2)(2l+m) ) = m C / (A B).

Thus, (M_x - N_x)^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4 M_x N_x = (4 l^2 C^2)/(A^2 B^2) - 4 m C/(A B) = 4 C/(A B) [ l^2 C/(A B) - m ] = 4 C/(A B) * ( l^2 C - m A B )/(A B) = 4 C ( l^2 C - m A B )/(A^2 B^2).

So (M_x - N_x)^2 = 4 C ( l^2 C - m A B )/(A^2 B^2). Now, A = 1+l^2, B = 2l+m.

Compute l^2 C - m A B = l^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1) - m (1+l^2)(2l+m). Expand:

l^2 (2 l m + m^2 - 1) = 2 l^3 m + l^2 m^2 - l^2.
m (1+l^2)(2l+m) = m (2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m) = 2 l m + m^2 + 2 l^3 m + l^2 m^2.
So l^2 C - m A B = (2 l^3 m + l^2 m^2 - l^2) - (2 l m + m^2 + 2 l^3 m + l^2 m^2) = 2 l^3 m + l^2 m^2 - l^2 - 2 l m - m^2 - 2 l^3 m - l^2 m^2 = - l^2 - 2 l m - m^2 = -(l^2 + 2 l m + m^2) = -(l+m)^2.

Thus, l^2 C - m A B = -(l+m)^2.

Therefore, (M_x - N_x)^2 = 4 C * ( -(l+m)^2 )/(A^2 B^2) = -4 C (l+m)^2/(A^2 B^2). But note that (M_x - N_x)^2 is non-negative, so the right-hand side must be non-negative. That means C must be negative (since (l+m)^2>0). Indeed, C = 2 l m + m^2 - 1. In our numeric, l is slope of L? In numeric, we had L: y = lx + m. In the M=0,N=1 system, we had A=(0,0), P=(p,0) with p=1? Actually, we set p=1. In that case, we had l and m. For numeric, we had earlier M=4,N=5 in original? That was different. But anyway, C is negative, so -4C is positive. So the expression is fine.

Now, we need to show that H_y^2 + V H_y = -4 C (l+m)^2/(A^2 B^2). Let's compute H_y^2 + V H_y using our expressions.

We have:

H_y = (1 - m^2)/(l+m) + (C Q)/(A B (l+m)) = (1 - m^2)/(l+m) + (C Q)/(A B (l+m)).

Let’s denote D = l+m. Then H_y = (1 - m^2)/D + (C Q)/(A B D). So H_y = [ (1 - m^2) A B + C Q ]/(A B D). So H_y = (1 - m^2) A B + C Q / (A B D). But note that (1 - m^2) A B = (1-m^2)(1+l^2)(2l+m). So H_y = [ (1-m^2)A B + C Q ]/(A B D).

Now, V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B). So V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B).

Now, compute H_y^2 + V H_y = H_y (H_y + V). So we need to compute H_y + V.

H_y + V = [ (1-m^2)A B + C Q ]/(A B D) - 2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B) = [ (1-m^2)A B + C Q - 2m A B D - 2C(1-l^2) D ]/(A B D).

Because -2m = -2m * (A B D)/(A B D) and -2C(1-l^2)/(A B) = -2C(1-l^2) D/(A B D). So numerator of H_y+V = (1-m^2)A B + C Q - 2m A B D - 2C(1-l^2) D.

Now, D = l+m. Also, A = 1+l^2, B = 2l+m.

We also have Q = 2l + m + l^2 m. And C = 2 l m + m^2 - 1.

We need to compute the numerator and then H_y (H_y+V) = [ (1-m^2)A B + C Q ]/(A B D) * [ numerator ]/(A B D) = [ (1-m^2)A B + C Q ] * [ numerator ]/(A^2 B^2 D^2). Then we need to show that this equals -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2). Because (l+m)^2 = D^2. So we need:

[ (1-m^2)A B + C Q ] * [ numerator ]/(A^2 B^2 D^2) = -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2) => [ (1-m^2)A B + C Q ] * [ numerator ] = -4 C D^4.

Thus, we need to verify that:

[ (1-m^2)A B + C Q ] * [ (1-m^2)A B + C Q - 2m A B D - 2C(1-l^2) D ] = -4 C D^4.

This is a polynomial identity in l,m. Let's try to simplify the left-hand side.

Let’s denote X = (1-m^2)A B + C Q. Then left side = X * (X - 2m A B D - 2C(1-l^2) D). So we need to show that X^2 - 2D A B (m + (1-l^2)?? Actually, careful: X - 2m A B D - 2C(1-l^2) D = X - 2D ( m A B + C(1-l^2) ). So left side = X^2 - 2D X ( m A B + C(1-l^2) ). So we need to show:

X^2 - 2D X ( m A B + C(1-l^2) ) = -4 C D^4.

This is a quadratic in X. If we can find that X = something like? Let's try to compute X explicitly.

We have A = 1+l^2, B = 2l+m, D = l+m.

Compute (1-m^2)A B = (1-m^2)(1+l^2)(2l+m). And C Q = (2 l m + m^2 - 1)(2l + m + l^2 m). So X = (1-m^2)(1+l^2)(2l+m) + (2 l m + m^2 - 1)(2l + m + l^2 m). This looks like it might factor as something like (l+m)^2? Let's try to compute X using polynomial expansion. This is doable but messy. However, note that the identity we need to prove is symmetric in some sense. Perhaps there is a simpler geometric interpretation: We have derived that (M_x - N_x)^2 = -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2). And we also have that H_y = X/(A B D). Then the condition for tangency is H_y^2 + V H_y = (M_x - N_x)^2. So we need to show that (X/(A B D))^2 + V (X/(A B D)) = -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2). Multiply both sides by A^2 B^2 D^2: X^2 + V X A B D = -4 C D^4. But V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B). So V X A B D = (-2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B)) X A B D = -2m X A B D - 2C(1-l^2) X D. So the equation becomes:

X^2 - 2m X A B D - 2C(1-l^2) X D = -4 C D^4. This matches our earlier expression.

Now, we can try to compute X in terms of l,m. Let's do that systematically.

First, compute (1-m^2)(1+l^2)(2l+m). Expand (1+l^2)(2l+m) = (2l+m) + l^2(2l+m) = 2l+m + 2l^3 + l^2 m. So = 2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m. Then multiply by (1-m^2): = (1-m^2)(2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m) = (2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m) - m^2(2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m) = 2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m - 2l m^2 - m^3 - 2l^3 m^2 - l^2 m^3.

Now, compute C Q: C = 2 l m + m^2 - 1, Q = 2l + m + l^2 m. So C Q = (2 l m + m^2 - 1)(2l + m + l^2 m). Expand:

Let’s denote U = 2l + m + l^2 m. Then (2 l m)(U) = 2l m (2l + m + l^2 m) = 4 l^2 m + 2 l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2.
(m^2)(U) = m^2(2l + m + l^2 m) = 2 l m^2 + m^3 + l^2 m^3.
(-1)(U) = -2l - m - l^2 m.

So C Q = (4 l^2 m + 2 l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2) + (2 l m^2 + m^3 + l^2 m^3) + (-2l - m - l^2 m) = Combine:

4 l^2 m + 2 l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2 + 2 l m^2 + m^3 + l^2 m^3 - 2l - m - l^2 m.

Group like terms:

l terms: -2l.
m terms: -m.
l^2 m terms: 4 l^2 m - l^2 m = 3 l^2 m.
l m^2 terms: 2 l m^2 + 2 l m^2 = 4 l m^2.
l^3 m^2 terms: 2 l^3 m^2.
l^2 m^3 terms: l^2 m^3.
m^3 term: m^3.

So C Q = -2l - m + 3 l^2 m + 4 l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2 + l^2 m^3 + m^3.

Now, add the first part: (1-m^2)A B = 2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m - 2l m^2 - m^3 - 2l^3 m^2 - l^2 m^3. So X = sum:

X = [2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m - 2l m^2 - m^3 - 2l^3 m^2 - l^2 m^3] + [ -2l - m + 3 l^2 m + 4 l m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2 + l^2 m^3 + m^3 ].

Now, combine term by term:

2l - 2l = 0.
m - m = 0.
2l^3 + 0 = 2l^3.
l^2 m + 3 l^2 m = 4 l^2 m.
-2l m^2 + 4 l m^2 = 2 l m^2.
- m^3 + m^3 = 0.
-2l^3 m^2 + 2 l^3 m^2 = 0.
- l^2 m^3 + l^2 m^3 = 0.

Thus, X = 2l^3 + 4 l^2 m + 2 l m^2 = 2l (l^2 + 2 l m + m^2) = 2l (l+m)^2 = 2l D^2.

Great! So X = 2l D^2.

Thus, H_y = X/(A B D) = (2l D^2)/(A B D) = 2l D/(A B). So H_y = 2l D/(A B). That's a very nice simplification!

Now, we also have V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B). But we might not need V explicitly if we can compute H_y^2 + V H_y directly using the expression for H_y and the relation we need. We have H_y = 2l D/(A B). Now, we need to verify that H_y^2 + V H_y = (M_x - N_x)^2 = -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2). Since H_y = 2l D/(A B), we have H_y^2 = 4 l^2 D^2/(A^2 B^2). Then H_y^2 + V H_y = 4 l^2 D^2/(A^2 B^2) + V * (2l D/(A B)). So we need:

4 l^2 D^2/(A^2 B^2) + 2l D V/(A B) = -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2).

Multiply both sides by A^2 B^2:

4 l^2 D^2 + 2l D V A B = -4 C D^2.

Thus, 2l D V A B = -4 D^2 (l^2 + C). So V = -2 D (l^2 + C)/(l A B). But we also have V from definition. So we need to check that this relation holds given the definitions of l,m,p=1 and the expressions for C and A,B,D. Let's compute l^2 + C = l^2 + (2 l m + m^2 - 1) = l^2 + 2 l m + m^2 - 1 = (l+m)^2 - 1 = D^2 - 1. So then V = -2 D (D^2 - 1)/(l A B). Now, A B = (1+l^2)(2l+m). So V = -2 D (D^2 - 1)/(l (1+l^2)(2l+m)). We also have from earlier: V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B). So we need to verify that these two expressions are equal. That is:

-2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B) = -2 D (D^2 - 1)/(l A B). Multiply both sides by -1/2: m + C(1-l^2)/(A B) = D (D^2 - 1)/(l A B). So we need to show:

m A B + C(1-l^2) = D (D^2 - 1)/l.

Now, D = l+m. Let's compute both sides.

Left side: m A B + C(1-l^2) = m (1+l^2)(2l+m) + (2 l m + m^2 - 1)(1-l^2). We already computed something like this? Actually, we computed X = (1-m^2)A B + C Q = 2l D^2. But now we have m A B + C(1-l^2). Let's compute that.

Compute A B = (1+l^2)(2l+m) = 2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m.

So m A B = m(2l + m + 2l^3 + l^2 m) = 2l m + m^2 + 2l^3 m + l^2 m^2.

Now, C(1-l^2) = (2 l m + m^2 - 1)(1-l^2) = (2 l m + m^2 - 1) - (2 l m + m^2 - 1) l^2 = (2 l m + m^2 - 1) - (2 l^3 m + l^2 m^2 - l^2). So = 2 l m + m^2 - 1 - 2 l^3 m - l^2 m^2 + l^2.

Thus, left side = m A B + C(1-l^2) = (2l m + m^2 + 2l^3 m + l^2 m^2) + (2 l m + m^2 - 1 - 2 l^3 m - l^2 m^2 + l^2) = Combine:

2l m + 2l m = 4l m.
m^2 + m^2 = 2m^2.
2l^3 m - 2l^3 m = 0.
l^2 m^2 - l^2 m^2 = 0.
Then -1 + l^2.

So left side = 4l m + 2m^2 + l^2 - 1.

Now, right side: D (D^2 - 1)/l = (l+m)((l+m)^2 - 1)/l = (l+m)(l^2 + 2lm + m^2 - 1)/l = (l+m)(l^2 + 2lm + m^2 - 1)/l.

We need to check if 4l m + 2m^2 + l^2 - 1 equals that. Compute (l+m)(l^2 + 2lm + m^2 - 1) = (l+m)[ (l+m)^2 - 1] = (l+m)[(l+m-1)(l+m+1)] = (l+m)(l+m-1)(l+m+1). Expand: (l+m)(l+m-1)(l+m+1) = (l+m)[(l+m)^2 - 1] = (l+m)(l^2+2lm+m^2-1). Now, we need to see if this equals l*(4l m + 2m^2 + l^2 - 1). Let's compute l*(4l m + 2m^2 + l^2 - 1) = 4l^2 m + 2l m^2 + l^3 - l. And (l+m)(l^2+2lm+m^2-1) = (l+m)(l^2+2lm+m^2) - (l+m) = (l+m)(l+m)^2 - (l+m) = (l+m)^3 - (l+m) = (l^3+3l^2 m+3l m^2+m^3) - l - m = l^3+3l^2 m+3l m^2+m^3 - l - m. So we need to check if l^3+3l^2 m+3l m^2+m^3 - l - m equals 4l^2 m + 2l m^2 + l^3 - l. That would require 3l^2 m+3l m^2+m^3 - m = 4l^2 m + 2l m^2 => bring all: 3l^2 m -4l^2 m + 3l m^2 -2l m^2 + m^3 - m =0 => -l^2 m + l m^2 + m^3 - m =0 => m(-l^2 + l m + m^2 -1)=0 => m( -l^2 + l m + m^2 -1)=0. But from C = 2lm+m^2-1, we have -l^2 + lm + m^2 -1 =? Not necessarily zero. So it seems that the equality does not hold identically. Wait, we must have made a mistake: The equation we derived from the tangency condition was 4 l^2 D^2 + 2l D V A B = -4 C D^2. That came from multiplying by A^2 B^2 D^2? Let's double-check that derivation.

We have H_y = 2l D/(A B). Then H_y^2 + V H_y = (2l D/(A B))^2 + V * (2l D/(A B)). We need this to equal (M_x - N_x)^2 = -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2). So:

4 l^2 D^2/(A^2 B^2) + (2l D V)/(A B) = -4 C D^2/(A^2 B^2).

Multiply both sides by A^2 B^2:

4 l^2 D^2 + 2l D V A B = -4 C D^2.

Thus, 2l D V A B = -4 D^2 (l^2 + C). So V = -2 D (l^2 + C)/(l A B). That is correct.

Now, we have an expression for V from the definition: V = -2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B). So we need to check if these two expressions are equal. That is:

-2m - 2C(1-l^2)/(A B) = -2 D (l^2 + C)/(l A B). Multiply both sides by -1/2: m + C(1-l^2)/(A B) = D (l^2 + C)/(l A B). Multiply by A B: m A B + C(1-l^2) = D (l^2 + C)/l.

So we need to verify that m A B + C(1-l^2) = (l+m)(l^2 + C)/l. Let's compute (l+m)(l^2 + C)/l. Since C = 2lm+m^2-1, then l^2+C = l^2+2lm+m^2-1 = (l+m)^2-1 = D^2-1. So (l+m)(D^2-1)/l = D (D^2-1)/l. So we need to show:

m A B + C(1-l^2) = D (D^2-1)/l.

Now, we computed left side as 4l m + 2m^2 + l^2 - 1. And right side as D (D^2-1)/l = (l+m)((l+m)^2-1)/l = (l+m)(l^2+2lm+m^2-1)/l. So we need to check if:

4l m + 2m^2 + l^2 - 1 = (l+m)(l^2+2lm+m^2-1)/l.

Compute right side: (l+m)(l^2+2lm+m^2-1)/l = (l+m)[(l+m)^2-1]/l = (l+m)(l+m-1)(l+m+1)/l.

Now, multiply both sides by l: l(4l m + 2m^2 + l^2 - 1) = (l+m)(l+m-1)(l+m+1). Left side: 4l^2 m + 2l m^2 + l^3 - l. Right side: (l+m)^3 - (l+m) = l^3+3l^2 m+3l m^2+m^3 - l - m. So we need:

4l^2 m + 2l m^2 + l^3 - l = l^3+3l^2 m+3l m^2+m^3 - l - m.

Cancel l^3: 4l^2 m + 2l m^2 - l = 3l^2 m + 3l m^2 + m^3 - l - m.

Bring all to one side: 4l^2 m + 2l m^2 - l - 3l^2 m - 3l m^2 - m^3 + l + m = 0 => (4l^2 m - 3l^2 m) + (2l m^2 - 3l m^2) + (-l+l) + m - m^3 = 0 => l^2 m - l m^2 + m - m^3 = 0 => m(l^2 - l m + 1 - m^2) = 0 => m( (l^2 - m^2) - l m + 1 ) = 0 => m( (l-m)(l+m) - l m + 1 ) = 0. Therefore, our assumption that the tangency condition is equivalent to H_y^2+V H_y = (M_x-N_x)^2 might be wrong. Let's re-examine the condition for tangency.

We have the circle (BEF). Its center O = (-U/2, -V/2) = (M_x+N_x, -V/2). The radius R_circ = sqrt( (U^2+V^2)/4 - W ). We want to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to this circle. M_x+N_x = -2l C/(A B)

Divide 4: l D [ l D - C(1-l^2) ] - m l D A B = - C D^2.

Factor l D: l D ( l D - C(1-l^2) - m A B ) = - C D^2.

Thus, we need:

l D ( l D - C(1-l^2) - m A B ) = - C D^2.

If D ≠ 0, we can divide by D: l ( l D - C(1-l^2) - m A B ) = - C D. So:

l^2 D - l C(1-l^2) - l m A B = - C D => Bring all: l^2 D + C D - l C(1-l^2) - l m A B = 0 => D(l^2 + C) - l [ C(1-l^2) + m A B ] = 0 => D(l^2 + C) = l ( m A B + C(1-l^2) ). This is the same equation we derived.

Now, we need to check if this equation is true given the definitions of l,m and the relation between A,B,C,D. Let's compute the right-hand side using our expressions for A,B,C,D. We have:

A = 1+l^2.
B = 2l+m.
C = 2lm + m^2 - 1.

Altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. PN vector = (N_x-1, lN_x+m). A perpendicular vector is (lN_x+m, 1-N_x). So altitude from M: (M_x + u(lN_x+m), M_y + u(1-N_x)). Set equal to H:

1+lt = M_x + u(lN_x+m)
-t = M_y + u(1-N_x)

From second: t = -M_y - u(1-N_x). Substitute into first:

1 + l(-M_y - u(1-N_x)) = M_x + u(lN_x+m)
=> 1 - l M_y - l u (1-N_x) = M_x + u(lN_x+m)
=> 1 - l M_y - M_x = u(lN_x+m + l(1-N_x)) = u(lN_x+m + l - lN_x) = u(l+m)

Thus, u = (1 - l M_y - M_x) / (l+m). Then H_y = -t = M_y + u(1-N_x). So H_y = M_y + (1-N_x) * (1 - l M_y - M_x) / (l+m).

Now, we need to express this in terms of l,m. We have M_y = l M_x + m. So 1 - l M_y - M_x = 1 - l(l M_x + m) - M_x = 1 - l^2 M_x - l m - M_x = 1 - l m - (l^2+1)M_x = 1 - l m - A M_x, where A=1+l^2.

Thus, u = (1 - l m - A M_x) / D, with D=l+m.

Then H_y = l M_x + m + (1-N_x) * (1 - l m - A M_x) / D.

Now, we also have that M_x and N_x are the two roots of the quadratic equation we derived earlier. In fact, from the quadratic for M_x (or N_x) we have that the values M_x and N_x satisfy A M_x^2 + (1 - 2l m?) Wait, we need an equation relating M_x to l,m. Actually, we derived that M_x and N_x are the two roots of the quadratic: A y^2 + B y + C = 0? No, we derived that the values y = M_x, N_x satisfy the quadratic: (1+l^2) y^2 + 2(lm-1) y + m^2? That was for x, not for y. We computed that for y, the coefficients are: Coeff_y2 = m(1+l^2)(2l+m), Coeff_y = 2l m (2l m + m^2 -1), Const = m^2 (2l m + m^2 -1). -2m * (2l D/(AB)) = -4l m D/(AB).
- 2C(1-l^2)/(AB) * (2m^2/D) = -4C(1-l^2) m^2/(AB D).

Multiply both sides by D^2 to clear denominators:

4m^4 + D [ 4m^2 (2l - C(1-l^2))/(AB) - 4m^3 ] + 4l D^3/(A^2 B^2) [ l D - C(1-l^2) ] = -4C D^4/(A^2 B^2).

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a more clever synthetic proof. Let's try to find a synthetic proof using properties of the orthocenter and the radical axis.

Consider the circle with diameter AP. Since P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD, we have PA=PC=PD. The circle with diameter AP is tangent to ω at A. Also, it passes through the midpoint of AP. Let’s denote Q as the midpoint of AP. Then the circle with diameter AP has center Q. Since AP is a radius of ω, we have that AQ = QP = AP/2. Also, the tangent to ω at A is perpendicular to AP. That tangent line also passes through the center Q? No, the tangent at A to ω is perpendicular to AP, so it is parallel to the tangent to the circle with diameter AP at A? Actually, at A, both circles have the same tangent line because they are tangent to each other. So the tangent line at A to both circles is perpendicular to AP. So it is the line through A perpendicular to AP.

Now, consider point H. Since H is on the line through P perpendicular to MN, and we have that the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN, which is l_P. So H lies on l_P. Also, the line through H parallel to AP is some line. We want to show that this line is tangent to the circumcircle of BEF.

Observe that E and F are on AP. So the points B, E, F are such that B is the reflection of A across MN. Since AP is a line through A, the reflection of AP across MN is a line through B that is parallel to AP? Actually, reflection across a line maps a line through A to a line through B that is the reflection of the line. Since A and B are symmetric about MN, the reflection of any line through A is a line through B. In particular, the reflection of line AP (through A) is a line through B that is the mirror image. Since AP is not necessarily perpendicular to MN, its reflection will be some line through B. However, the line through H parallel to AP might be that reflected line? Not necessarily.

But note that H lies on l_P, which is perpendicular to MN. So H is on the line through P perpendicular to MN. Also, AP is some line through A. The line through H parallel to AP is not necessarily the reflection of AP.

Another idea: Use the fact that the circle (BEF) is the circle through B and the two points where AP meets the circles. Since B is the reflection of A across MN, there is a homothety centered at the intersection of the circles? Not.

Given the time, I'll try to produce a synthetic proof based on the following: Let O be the circumcenter of triangle BEF. We have shown that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF, which is the line through the midpoint of EF perpendicular to AP. Since E and F are on AP, the perpendicular bisector of EF is a line through the midpoint of EF that is perpendicular to AP. That line is also the line through the midpoint of EF that is parallel to the line through P perpendicular to AP? Not.

But we also have that H lies on the line through P perpendicular to MN. And we know that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circle (BEF). That means that the distance from O to that line equals the radius. Perhaps we can prove that O is the reflection of H across the midpoint of EF? Or something.

Let's compute O in our coordinate system. We have B=(0,? Actually, in the rotated coordinate system, we have A=0, P=(1,0), L: y=lx+m. B is the reflection of A across L, so B = 2Q, with Q = foot from A to L. We found that Q = ( -lm/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) )? Actually, foot from origin to line y=lx+m is: ( -l m/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) ). So B = ( -2l m/(1+l^2), 2m/(1+l^2) ). So B = (b_x, b_y). And we have E=(2M_x,0), F=(2N_x,0). So the circumcenter O of triangle BEF: since E and F are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of EF is vertical line x = M_x+N_x. So O lies on x = M_x+N_x. Also, O lies on the perpendicular bisector of BE. So we can compute O_y from the condition that O is equidistant from B and E. Let O = (M_x+N_x, y_O). Then |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2. Compute:

|O-B|^2 = (M_x+N_x - b_x)^2 + (y_O - b_y)^2.
|O-E|^2 = (M_x+N_x - 2M_x)^2 + y_O^2 = (N_x - M_x)^2 + y_O^2.

Set equal:

(M_x+N_x - b_x)^2 + (y_O - b_y)^2 = (N_x - M_x)^2 + y_O^2.

The distance = |(O-H) × v| / |v|. Cross product magnitude (2D) = |(-0.405)*(-3.305) - (-9.35)*4.915| = |1.337 + 45.92| = 47.257. |v| = sqrt(35.08)=5.923. Distance = 47.257/5.923=7.98. That is approximately 8.00, which is the radius. So indeed L is tangent. So numeric verification works.

Now we need a rigorous proof.

Given the complexity, analytic geometry might be the most straightforward: compute everything in terms of m,n,h, then show that the distance from the circumcenter O of triangle BEF to line L equals the radius. Given the difficulty, maybe we should attempt an analytic proof with coordinates, but try to simplify using algebraic manipulations. Since we have parameters m,n,h, and r,R defined in terms of them, we can compute everything in terms of m,n,h. The expressions might simplify nicely if we use the relationships.

Let’s set:

- Let M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with 0 < m < n.
- Let A = (0,h) with h>0.
- Then r = MA = √(m^2+h^2), R = NA = √(n^2+h^2).
- C = (m - r, 0) because on Ω on the ray from M opposite N. So C_x = m - r.
- D = (n + R, 0) because on Γ on the ray from N opposite M. So D_x = n + R.
- P is circumcenter of ACD. Since C and D have y=0, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line through midpoint: x = ( (m - r) + (n + R) )/2 = (m + n - r + R)/2. So p_x = (m + n - r + R)/2.
- p_y is found from perpendicular bisector of AC. A=(0,h), C=(m - r,0). Midpoint AC: ((m - r)/2, h/2). Slope AC: (0 - h)/( (m - r) - 0 ) = -h/(m - r) = h/(r - m) (since m - r negative, r - m positive). So perpendicular slope = -(r - m)/h. So line: y - h/2 = -(r - m)/h (x - (m - r)/2). At x = p_x, we get y = h/2 - (r - m)/h (p_x - (m - r)/2). Compute p_x - (m - r)/2 = (m+n - r + R)/2 - (m - r)/2 = (n + R)/2. So p_y = h/2 - (r - m)(n+R)/(2h). So p_y = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R))/(2h). This is correct.

Now define v = P - A = (p_x, p_y - h). Compute p_y - h = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R))/(2h) - h = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R) - 2h^2)/(2h) = -(h^2 + (r - m)(n+R))/(2h). So v = (p_x, - (h^2 + (r - m)(n+R))/(2h) ). Let’s denote T = h^2 + (r - m)(n+R). Then v = (p_x, -T/(2h)). So v_y negative.

Now we need E and F. As derived, t_E = (2m p_x + T)/|v|^2, t_F = (2n p_x + T)/|v|^2, where |v|^2 = p_x^2 + T^2/(4h^2). Also note that B = (0,-h).

Now we want circle through B, E, F. Let's denote coordinates:

B = (0, -h)
E = (x_E, y_E) with x_E = t_E p_x, y_E = h - (t_E T)/(2h)
F = (x_F, y_F) with x_F = t_F p_x, y_F = h - (t_F T)/(2h)

We also have that t_E and t_F satisfy: from earlier, t_E = (2m p_x + T)/|v|^2, t_F = (2n p_x + T)/|v|^2. So these are known.

Given the heavy algebra, maybe we can use complex numbers with the line MN as real axis, and A as a point on the upper half-plane. Let’s set M=0, N=c (real), with c>0. Expand: (p+r)^2 + y^2 = (p-a)^2 + y^2 -2hy + h^2. Cancel y^2: (p+r)^2 = (p-a)^2 -2hy + h^2. So 2hy = (p-a)^2 + h^2 - (p+r)^2. So y = [(p-a)^2 + h^2 - (p+r)^2]/(2h). Simplify numerator: (p-a)^2 - (p+r)^2 + h^2 = ((p-a)-(p+r))((p-a)+(p+r)) + h^2 = (-a - r)(2p - a + r) + h^2 = -(a+r)(2p - a + r) + h^2. But note that p = (c+R-r)/2, so 2p = c+R-r. Then 2p - a + r = c+R-r - a + r = c+R - a. So numerator = -(a+r)(c+R - a) + h^2. But we also have relations: a^2+h^2 = r^2, and (a-c)^2+h^2 = R^2. So h^2 = r^2 - a^2. Also, c+R - a =? So yes, midpoint of AE = foot from origin to line AP. So M_AE = A - (A·v)/|v|^2 v. Then E = 2 M_AE - A = A - 2 (A·v)/|v|^2 v. So consistent.

Similarly, for Γ with center N=(c,0), the midpoint of AF is the foot of perpendicular from N to line AP. So M_AF = A - ((A-c)·v)/|v|^2 v? Actually, foot from N to line: point on line A + t v such that (A + t v - N) · v = 0 => (A-N)·v + t |v|^2 = 0 => t = - (A-N)·v/|v|^2. So M_AF = A - ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2) v. Then F = 2 M_AF - A = A - 2 ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2 v.

Then the left side becomes: (1+α^2)a^2 + 2α a (h - α a) + (h^2 - 2α a h + α^2 a^2) = a^2 + α^2 a^2 + 2α a h - 2α^2 a^2 + h^2 - 2α a h + α^2 a^2 = a^2 + h^2 = r^2. So it's consistent. So the quadratic in x is: (1+α^2)x^2 + 2α b x + (b^2 - r^2)=0. Since x=a is a root, we can factor: (x-a)((1+α^2)x + (something)) = 0. The other root x_E satisfies: a + x_E = -2α b/(1+α^2). So x_E = -2α b/(1+α^2) - a. Then y_E = α x_E + b.

Similarly, for Γ: (x-c)^2 + (α x + b)^2 = R^2 => x^2 -2c x + c^2 + α^2 x^2 + 2α b x + b^2 = R^2 => (1+α^2)x^2 + (2α b - 2c)x + (c^2+b^2 - R^2)=0. Since A is on Γ, x=a is a root. So a + x_F = - (2α b - 2c)/(1+α^2) = 2(c - α b)/(1+α^2). So x_F = 2(c - α b)/(1+α^2) - a. Then y_F = α x_F + b.

Now, we have B = (a, -h). Note that -h is the negative of the y-coordinate of A. So B is the reflection of A across the x-axis (since the x-axis is line MN). Indeed, because the line of centers is the x-axis, and the common chord is vertical, so the intersection points are symmetric about the x-axis. So B = (a, -h). Good.

We can compute O as:

O = ( ( (x1^2+y1^2)(y2-y3) + (x2^2+y2^2)(y3-y1) + (x3^2+y3^2)(y1-y2) ) / (2*D), ( (x1^2+y1^2)(x3-x2) + (x2^2+y2^2)(x1-x3) + (x3^2+y3^2)(x2-x1) ) / (2*D) ), where D = x1(y2-y3)+x2(y3-y1)+x3(y1-y2). This is the standard formula using determinants. So we can compute U = -2x_O, V = -2y_O. That might be doable if we can simplify the expressions.

Now, note that b = h - α a. So α b = α h - α^2 a. Then 2α b/(1+α^2) = 2(α h - α^2 a)/(1+α^2) = 2α h/(1+α^2) - 2α^2 a/(1+α^2). Let's compute M explicitly using the condition M·C = |C|^2/2 and M lies on L. Since L is a line, we can param M = (t, l t + m). Then we have:
